<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Suffrage</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">Universal suffrage</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Right to vote", "Suffragist", and "The Franchise" redirect here. For the group, see <a href="/wiki/Right_to_Vote" title="Right to Vote">Right to Vote</a>. For the organisation, see <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Women%27s_Suffrage_Societies" title="National Union of Women's Suffrage Societies">National Union of Women's Suffrage Societies</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/The_Franchise_(disambiguation)" title="The Franchise (disambiguation)">The Franchise (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Right to vote in public and political elections</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2568" data-file-width="3476" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/300px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/450px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/600px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Suffrage universel dédié à <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Auguste_Ledru-Rollin" title="Alexandre Auguste Ledru-Rollin">Ledru-Rollin</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Sorrieu" title="Frédéric Sorrieu">Frédéric Sorrieu</a>, 1850</div></div></div>
<p><b>Suffrage</b>, <b>political franchise</b>, or simply <b>franchise</b>, is the <b>right to vote</b> in <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">public, political elections</a> and <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a> (although the term is sometimes used for any right to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vote" title="Vote">vote</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In some languages, and occasionally in English, the right to vote is called <b>active suffrage</b>, as distinct from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_suffrage" title="Passive suffrage">passive suffrage</a>, which is the right to stand for election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The combination of active and passive suffrage is sometimes called <i>full suffrage</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In most democracies, eligible voters can vote in elections of representatives. Voting on issues by referendum may also be available. For example, in Switzerland, this is permitted at all levels of government. In the United States, some <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">states</a> such as California, Washington, and Wisconsin have exercised their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shared_sovereignty" title="Shared sovereignty">shared sovereignty</a> to offer citizens the opportunity to write, propose, and vote on referendums; other states and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_federal_government" title="United States federal government">federal government</a> have not. <a href="/wiki/Referendums_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Referendums in the United Kingdom">Referendums in the United Kingdom</a> are rare.
</p><p>Suffrage is granted to everybody mentally capable, in most countries defined by <a href="/wiki/Voting_age" title="Voting age">voting age</a>, and being citizen of the country. In some countries additional restrictions exist. In the Great Britain and United States a felon may not vote. As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Florida felons with court debts may not vote. In some countries being under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guardianship" title="Guardianship">guardianship</a> may restrict the right to vote. Resident <a href="/wiki/Right_of_foreigners_to_vote" title="Right of foreigners to vote">non-citizens can vote</a> in some countries, which may be restricted to citizens of closely linked countries (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations#Commonwealth_citizenship_and_high_commissioners" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth citizens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elections_to_the_European_Parliament#Eligibility" title="Elections to the European Parliament">European Union citizens</a>) or to certain offices or questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Historically the right to vote was more restricted, for example by gender, race, or wealth.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>suffrage</i> comes from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>suffragium</i>, which initially meant "a voting-tablet", "a ballot", "a vote", or "the right to vote". <i>Suffragium</i> in the second century and later came to mean "political patronage, influence, interest, or support", and sometimes "popular acclaim" or "applause". By the fourth century the word was used for "an intercession", asking a patron for their influence with the Almighty. <i>Suffragium</i> was used in the fifth and sixth centuries with connection to buying influence or profiteering from appointing to office, and eventually the word referred to the bribe itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> William Smith rejects the connection of <i>suffragium</i> to <i>sub</i> "under" + <i>fragor</i> "crash, din, shouts (as of approval)", related to <i>frangere</i> "to break"; Eduard Wunder writes that the word may be related to <i>suffrago</i>, signifying an ankle bone or knuckle bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latin_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-latin-10">[10]</a></sup> In the 17th century the English <i>suffrage</i> regained the earlier meaning of the Latin <i>suffragium</i>, "a vote" or "the right to vote".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_suffrage">Universal suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Universal suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">Universal suffrage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peterloo_Massacre.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Peterloo_Massacre.png/220px-Peterloo_Massacre.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Peterloo_Massacre.png/330px-Peterloo_Massacre.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Peterloo_Massacre.png/440px-Peterloo_Massacre.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peterloo_Massacre.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_Massacre" title="Peterloo Massacre">Peterloo Massacre</a> of 1819</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">Universal suffrage</a> consists of the right to vote without restriction due to gender, race, religion, social status, education level, or wealth. It typically does not extend the right to vote to all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residency_(domicile)" title="Residency (domicile)">residents</a> of a region as distinctions are still frequently made in regard to <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenship</a>, age, and occasionally mental capacity or criminal convictions.
</p><p>The short-lived <a href="/wiki/Corsican_Republic" title="Corsican Republic">Corsican Republic</a> (1755–1769) was the first country to grant limited universal suffrage to all citizens over the age of 25.
</p><p>In 1819 60–80,000 men and women from 30 miles around Manchester assembled in the city's <a href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Square,_Manchester" title="St Peter's Square, Manchester">St. Peter's Square</a> to protest their lack of any representation in the Houses of Parliament. Historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Poole_(historian)" title="Robert Poole (historian)">Robert Poole</a> has called the <a href="/wiki/Peterloo_Massacre" title="Peterloo Massacre">Peterloo Massacre</a> one of the defining moments of its age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> (The eponymous <i><a href="/wiki/Peterloo_(film)" title="Peterloo (film)">Peterloo</a></i> film featured a scene of women suffragists planning their contribution to the protest.) At that time Manchester had a population of around 140,000 and the population totals of <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_Greater_Manchester" title="Demography of Greater Manchester">Greater Manchester</a> were around 490,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>This was followed by other experiments in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a> of 1871 and the island republic of <a href="/wiki/Franceville,_New_Hebrides" title="Franceville, New Hebrides">Franceville</a> (1889). From 1840 to 1852, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawai%27i" title="Kingdom of Hawai'i">Kingdom of Hawai'i</a> granted universal suffrage without mention of sex. In 1893, when the Kingdom of Hawai'i was <a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_the_Hawaiian_Kingdom" title="Overthrow of the Hawaiian Kingdom">overthrown in a coup</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> was the only independent country to practice universal (active) suffrage, and the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a> index lists New Zealand as the only free country in the world in 1893.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Women.27s_suffrage"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_suffrage">Women's suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Women's suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1150" data-file-width="873" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg/220px-SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg/330px-SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg/440px-SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SPD-Plakat_1919.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German election poster from 1919: <i>Equal rights – equal duties!</i></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">Women's suffrage</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">Women's suffrage</a> is, by definition, the right of women to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This was the goal of the suffragists, who believed in using legal means, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffragettes</a>, who used extremist measures. Short-lived suffrage equity was drafted into provisions of the State of New Jersey's first, 1776 Constitution, which extended the Right to Vote to unwed female landholders and black land owners.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
"IV. That all inhabitants of this Colony, of full age, who are worth fifty pounds proclamation money, clear estate in the same, and have resided within the county in which they claim a vote for twelve months immediately preceding the election, shall be entitled to vote for Representatives in Council and Assembly; and also for all other public officers, that shall be elected by the people of the county at large." <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> <i>1776</i></p></blockquote>
<p>However, the document did not specify an Amendment procedure, and the provision was subsequently replaced in 1844 by the adoption of the <a href="/wiki/1844_New_Jersey_Constitution" title="1844 New Jersey Constitution">succeeding constitution</a>, which reverted to "all white male" suffrage restrictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Kingdom of Hawai'i granted female suffrage in 1840, the right was rescinded in 1852. Limited voting rights were gained by some women in Sweden, Britain, and some western U.S. states in the 1860s. In 1893, the British colony of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> became the first self-governing nation to extend the right to vote to all adult women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1894, the women of <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> achieved the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Amendment_(Adult_Suffrage)_Act_1894" title="Constitutional Amendment (Adult Suffrage) Act 1894">right to both vote and stand for Parliament</a>. The autonomous <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> was the first nation to allow all women to both vote and run for parliament.
</p>
<h4><span id="Anti-women.27s_suffrage_propaganda"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-women's_suffrage_propaganda">Anti-women's suffrage propaganda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Anti-women's suffrage propaganda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3915" data-file-width="2508" decoding="async" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg/220px-Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg/330px-Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg/440px-Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anti_Suffrage_Postcard_c.1908_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British postcard against women's suffrage postcard from c.1908. It shows unflattering <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricatures</a> of suffragettes in front of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">parliament</a> and the caption: 'This is the house that man built' with a poem. From the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_History_Museum" title="People's History Museum">People's History Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Those against the women's suffrage movement made public organizations to put down the political movement, with the main argument being that a woman's place was in the home, not polls. Political cartoons and public outrage over women's rights increased as the opposition to suffrage worked to organize legitimate groups campaigning against women's voting rights. The Massachusetts Association Opposed to the Further Extension of Suffrage to Women was one organization that came out of the 1880s to put down the voting efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many anti-suffrage propaganda poked fun at the idea of women in politics. Political cartoons displayed the most sentiment by portraying the issue of women's suffrage to be swapped with men's lives. Some mocked the popular suffrage hairstyle of full-upward combed hair. Others depicted young girls turning into suffragettes after a failure in life, such as not being married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equal_suffrage">Equal suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Equal suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Weighted_voting" title="Weighted voting">Weighted voting</a></div>
<p>Equal suffrage is sometimes confused with <i>Universal suffrage</i>, although the meaning of the former is the removal of graded votes, wherein a voter could possess a number of votes in accordance with income, wealth or social status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Census_suffrage">Census suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Census suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Also known as "censitary suffrage", it is the opposite of <i>equal suffrage,</i> meaning that the votes cast by those eligible to vote are not equal, but weighed differently according to the person's income or rank in society (e.g., people who don't own property or whose income is lower than a given amount are barred from voting; or people with higher education have more votes than those with lower education; stockholders who have more shares in a given company have more votes than those with fewer shares). In many countries, census suffrage restricted who could vote and be elected: in the United States, until the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian reforms</a> of the 1830's, only men who owned land of a specified acreage or monetary value could vote or participate in elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Similarly, in Brazil, the Constitution of 1824 established that, in order to vote, citizens would need to have an annual income of 200,000 Milreis and, to be voted, their minimum annual income would need to be 400,000 Milreis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compulsory_suffrage">Compulsory suffrage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Compulsory suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_suffrage" title="Compulsory suffrage">Compulsory suffrage</a></div>
<p>Where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_suffrage" title="Compulsory suffrage">compulsory suffrage</a> exists, those who are eligible to vote are required by law to do so. Thirty-two countries currently practise this form of suffrage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_vote">Business vote</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Business vote">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Business_vote"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plural_voting" title="Plural voting">Plural voting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functional_constituency_(Hong_Kong)" title="Functional constituency (Hong Kong)">Functional constituency (Hong Kong)</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local government in England</a> and some of its ex-colonies, businesses formerly had, and in some places still have, a vote in the urban area in which they paid <a href="/wiki/Rates_(tax)" title="Rates (tax)">rates</a>. This is an extension of the historical property-based franchise from <a href="/wiki/Natural_person" title="Natural person">natural persons</a> to other <a href="/wiki/Legal_person" title="Legal person">legal persons</a>.
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporation_of_the_City_of_London" title="Corporation of the City of London">Corporation of the City of London</a> has retained and even expanded business vote, following the passing of the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London_(Ward_Elections)_Act_2002" title="City of London (Ward Elections) Act 2002">City of London (Ward Elections) Act 2002</a>. This has given business interests within the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a>, which is a major <a href="/wiki/Financial_centre" title="Financial centre">financial centre</a> with few residents, the opportunity to apply the accumulated wealth of the corporation to the development of an effective <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobby</a> for UK policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johal2012_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johal2012-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groundhog_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-groundhog-26">[26]</a></sup> This includes having the <a href="/wiki/City_Remembrancer" title="City Remembrancer">City Remembrancer</a>, financed by the <a href="/wiki/City%27s_Cash" title="City's Cash">City's Cash</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_agent" title="Parliamentary agent">parliamentary agent</a>, provided with a special seat in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> located in the under-gallery facing the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Commons_(United_Kingdom)" title="Speaker of the House of Commons (United Kingdom)">Speaker</a>'s chair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard-20140303_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansard-20140303-27">[27]</a></sup> In a leaked document from 2012, an official report concerning the City's Cash revealed that the aim of major occasions such as set-piece sumptious banquets featuring national politicians was "to increase the emphasis on complementing hospitality with business meetings consistent with the City corporation's role in supporting the City as a financial centre".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a_lesson_in_lobbying_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-a_lesson_in_lobbying-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first issue taken up by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_civil_rights_movement" title="Northern Ireland civil rights movement">Northern Ireland civil rights movement</a> was the business vote, abolished in 1968 (a year before it was abolished in Great Britain outside the City of London).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AugheyMorrow2014_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-AugheyMorrow2014-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, commercial ratepayers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[nb 1]</a></sup> can vote in local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebiscite" title="Plebiscite">plebiscites</a>, for changing the <a href="/wiki/Place_names_in_Ireland" title="Place names in Ireland">name of the locality or street</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[nb 2]</a></sup> or delimiting a <a href="/wiki/Business_improvement_district" title="Business improvement district">business improvement district</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> From 1930 to 1935, 5 of 35 members of <a href="/wiki/Dublin_City_Council" title="Dublin City Council">Dublin City Council</a> were "commercial members".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cities in most Australian states, voting is optional for businesses but compulsory for individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Basis_of_exclusion_from_suffrage">Basis of exclusion from suffrage<span class="anchor" id="Forms_of_exclusion_from_suffrage"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Basis of exclusion from suffrage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gender">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">Women's suffrage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="605" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg/220px-1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg/330px-1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg/440px-1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1912_Ohio_women_Headquarters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Women's Suffrage Headquarters on Euclid Avenue in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> in 1912</div></div></div>
<p>In ancient <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athens</a>, often cited as the birthplace of democracy, only adult, male citizens who owned land were permitted to vote. Through subsequent centuries, Europe was generally ruled by monarchs, though various forms of parliament arose at different times. The high rank ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbesses</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> permitted some women the right to sit and vote at national assemblies – as with various high-ranking abbesses in Medieval Germany, who were ranked among the independent princes of the empire. Their Protestant successors enjoyed the same privilege almost into modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marie Guyart, a French nun who worked with the <a href="/wiki/First_Nations_in_Canada" title="First Nations in Canada">First Nations</a> peoples of Canada during the seventeenth century, wrote in 1654 regarding the suffrage practices of <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> women, "These female chieftains are women of standing amongst the savages, and they have a deciding vote in the councils. They make decisions there like the men, and it is they who even delegated the first ambassadors to discuss peace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> The Iroquois, like many First Nations peoples in North America, had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrilineal" title="Matrilineal">matrilineal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinship_system" title="Kinship system">kinship system</a>. Property and descent were passed through the female line. Women elders voted on hereditary male chiefs and could depose them.
</p><p>The emergence of modern democracy generally began with male citizens obtaining the right to vote in advance of female citizens, except in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawai%27i" title="Kingdom of Hawai'i">Kingdom of Hawai'i</a>, where universal suffrage without mention of age or sex was introduced in 1840; however, a constitutional amendment in 1852 rescinded female voting and put property qualifications on male voting.
</p><p>Voting rights for women were introduced into <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a> by the United Nations' Human Rights Commission, whose elected chair was <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>. In 1948 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>; Article 21 stated: "(1) Everyone has the right to take part in the government of his country, directly or through freely chosen representatives. (3) The will of the people shall be the basis of the authority of government; this will shall be expressed in periodic and genuine elections which shall be by universal and equal suffrage and shall be held by secret vote or by equivalent free voting procedures."
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Political_Rights_of_Women" title="Convention on the Political Rights of Women">Convention on the Political Rights of Women</a>, which went into force in 1954, enshrining the equal rights of women to vote, hold office, and access public services as set out by national laws. One of the most recent jurisdictions to acknowledge women's full right to vote was <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_Bhutanese_general_election" title="2008 Bhutanese general election">2008</a> (its first national elections).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> Most recently, in 2011 <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Abdullah of Saudi Arabia">King Abdullah</a> of Saudi Arabia let women vote in the <a href="/wiki/2015_Saudi_Arabian_municipal_elections" title="2015 Saudi Arabian municipal elections">2015 local elections</a> (and from then on) and be appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Consultative_Assembly_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Consultative Assembly of Saudi Arabia">Consultative Assembly</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> it was common in European countries for people of disfavored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_denominations" title="Religious denominations">religious denominations</a> to be denied civil and political rights, often including the right to vote, to stand for election or to sit in parliament. In <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>, Roman Catholics were denied the right to vote from 1728 to 1793, and the right to sit in parliament until 1829. The anti-Catholic policy was justified on the grounds that the loyalty of Catholics supposedly lay with the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> rather than the national monarch.
</p><p>In England and Ireland, several Acts practically disenfranchised non-Anglicans or non-Protestants by imposing an oath before admission to vote or to stand for office. The 1672 and 1678 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test Acts</a> forbade non-Anglicans to hold public offices, and the 1727 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disenfranchising_Act" title="Disenfranchising Act">Disenfranchising Act</a> took away Catholics' voting rights in Ireland, which were restored only in 1788. Jews could not even be naturalized. An attempt was made to change this situation, but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Naturalization_Act_1753" title="Jewish Naturalization Act 1753">Jewish Naturalization Act 1753</a> provoked such reactions that it was repealed the following year. <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">Nonconformists</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodists" title="Methodists">Methodists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterians" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a>) were only allowed to run for election to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" title="British House of Commons">British House of Commons</a> starting in 1828, Catholics in 1829 (following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" title="Catholic Relief Act 1829">Catholic Relief Act 1829</a>, which extended the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Relief_Act_1791" title="Roman Catholic Relief Act 1791">Roman Catholic Relief Act 1791</a>), and Jews in 1858 (with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emancipation_of_the_Jews_in_England" title="Emancipation of the Jews in England">Emancipation of the Jews in England</a>). <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> could only begin his political career in 1837 because he had been converted to Anglicanism at the age of 12.
</p><p>In several states in the U.S. after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>, Jews, <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> or Catholics were denied voting rights and/or forbidden to run for office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamson1960_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-williamson1960-45">[43]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Constitution_of_1776" title="Delaware Constitution of 1776">Delaware Constitution of 1776</a> stated that "Every person who shall be chosen a member of either house, or appointed to any office or place of trust, before taking his seat, or entering upon the execution of his office, shall (…) also make and subscribe the following declaration, to wit: <i>I, A B. do profess faith in God the Father, and in Jesus Christ His only Son, and in the Holy Ghost, one God, blessed for evermore; and I do acknowledge the holy scriptures of the Old and New Testament to be given by divine inspiration.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> This was repealed by article I, section 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Constitution_of_1792" title="Delaware Constitution of 1792">1792 Constitution</a>: "No religious test shall be required as a qualification to any office, or public trust, under this State".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> The 1778 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Constitution" title="South Carolina Constitution">Constitution of the State of South Carolina</a> stated that "No person shall be eligible to sit in the house of representatives unless he be of the Protestant religion",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> the 1777 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)_Constitution" title="Georgia (U.S. state) Constitution">Constitution of the State of Georgia</a> (art. VI) that "The representatives shall be chosen out of the residents in each county (…) and they shall be of the Protestent <i>(sic)</i> religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, voting rights and eligibility were extended to Jews in 1828.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, several religious groups (<a href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">Mennonites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hutterites" title="Hutterites">Hutterites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doukhobors" title="Doukhobors">Doukhobors</a>) were disenfranchised by the wartime Elections Act of 1917, mainly because they opposed military service. This disenfranchisement ended with the closure of the First World War, but was renewed for Doukhobors from 1934 (via the <i>Dominion Elections Act</i>) to 1955.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Constitution of modern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> in 1866 provided in article 7 that only Christians could become Romanian citizens. <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Romania" title="History of the Jews in Romania">Jews native to Romania</a> were declared stateless persons. In 1879, under pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Berlin Peace Conference</a>, this article was amended, granting non-Christians the right to become Romanian citizens, but naturalization was granted on a case-by-case basis and was subject to Parliamentary approval. An application took over ten years to process. Only in 1923 was a new constitution adopted, whose article 133 extended Romanian citizenship to all Jewish residents and equality of rights to all Romanian citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wealth.2C_tax_class.2C_social_class"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wealth,_tax_class,_social_class">Wealth, tax class, social class</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Wealth, tax class, social class">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg/220px-Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg/330px-Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg/440px-Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manifestace_za_hlasovac%C3%AD_pr%C3%A1vo_1905.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demonstration for universal right to vote, Prague, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, 1905</div></div></div>
<p>Until the nineteenth century, many Western proto-democracies had <a href="/wiki/Property_qualification" title="Property qualification">property qualifications</a> in their electoral laws; e.g. only landowners could vote (because the only tax for such countries was the property tax), or the voting rights were weighted according to the amount of taxes paid (as in the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_three-class_franchise" title="Prussian three-class franchise">Prussian three-class franchise</a>). Most countries abolished the property qualification for national elections in the late nineteenth century, but retained it for local government elections for several decades. Today these laws have largely been abolished, although the <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homeless</a> may not be able to register because they lack regular addresses.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, until the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords_Act_1999" title="House of Lords Act 1999">House of Lords Act 1999</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peers</a> who were members of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> were excluded from voting for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> as they were not commoners. Although there is nothing to prevent the monarch from voting, it is considered improper for the monarch to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the 19th and 20th centuries, many nations made voters pay to elect officials, keeping impoverished people from being fully enfranchised. These laws were in effect in Argentina, Brazil, Canada, Chile, Costa Rica, Ecuador, Mexico, Peru, Uruguay, and Venezuela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge">Knowledge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Knowledge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sometimes the right to vote has been limited to people who had achieved a certain level of education or passed a certain test. In some US states, "<a href="/wiki/Literacy_test" title="Literacy test">literacy tests</a>" were previously implemented to exclude those who were illiterate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ourdocuments.gov_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ourdocuments.gov-55">[53]</a></sup> Black voters in the South were often deemed by election officials to have failed the test even when they did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> Under the 1961 constitution of <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, voting on the "A" roll, which elected up to 50 of the 65 members of parliament, was restricted based on education requirements, which in practice led to an overwhelming white vote. Voting on the "B" roll had universal suffrage, but only appointed 15 members of parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='Clarification needed: What is "A roll" and "B roll"? (December 2018)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, many countries other than the US placed voting restrictions on illiterate people, including: <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race">Race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various countries, usually countries with a dominant race within a wider population, have historically denied the vote to people of particular races, or to all but the dominant race. This has been achieved in a number of ways:
</p>
<ul><li>Official – laws and regulations passed specifically disenfranchising people of particular races (for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antebellum_United_States" title="Antebellum United States">Antebellum United States</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer_republic" title="Boer republic">Boer republics</a>, pre-apartheid and <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> South Africa, or many colonial political systems, who provided suffrage only for white settlers and some privileged non-white groups). Canada and Australia denied suffrage for their indigenous populations until the 1960s.</li>
<li>Indirect – nothing in law specifically prevents anyone from voting on account of their race, but other laws or regulations are used to exclude people of a particular race. In southern states of the United States of America before the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" title="Poll tax (United States)">poll taxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literacy_test" title="Literacy test">literacy</a> and other tests were used to disenfranchise African-Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ourdocuments.gov_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-ourdocuments.gov-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Property qualifications have tended to disenfranchise a minority race, particularly if tribally owned land is not allowed to be taken into consideration. In some cases this was an unintended (but usually welcome) consequence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many African colonies after World War II until decolonization had tough education and property qualifications which practically gave meaningful representation only for rich European minorities.</li>
<li>Unofficial – nothing in law prevents anyone from voting on account of their race, but people of particular races are intimidated or otherwise prevented from exercising this right. This was a common tactic employed by white Southerners against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedmen" title="Freedmen">Freedmen</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a> and the following period before more formal methods of disenfranchisement became entrenched. Unofficial discrimination could even manifest in ways which, while allowing the act of voting itself, effectively deprive it of any value – for example, in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, the country's <a href="/wiki/Arab_citizens_of_Israel" title="Arab citizens of Israel">Arab minority</a> has maintained a party-system separate from that of the Jewish majority. In the run-up for the country's <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=2015_Israeli_legsialtive_election&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="2015 Israeli legsialtive election (page does not exist)">2015 elections</a>, the electoral threshold was raised from 2% to 3.25%, thus forcing the dominant Arab parties – <a href="/wiki/Hadash" title="Hadash">Hadash</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_List" title="United Arab List">United Arab List</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balad_(political_party)" title="Balad (political party)">Balad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ta%27al" title="Ta'al">Ta'al</a> – either to run under <a href="/wiki/Joint_List" title="Joint List">one list</a> or risk losing their parliamentary representation.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age">Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;">Issues</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist" style="padding-top:0;">

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment_in_the_home" title="Corporal punishment in the home">at home</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Curfew" title="Curfew">Curfew</a></li>

.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Youth_rights_sidebar" title="Template:Youth rights sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Voting_age" title="Voting age">Voting age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Age_of_candidacy" title="Age of candidacy">Age of candidacy</a></div>
<p>All modern democracies require voters to meet age qualifications to vote. Worldwide voting ages are not consistent, differing between countries and even within countries, though the range usually varies between 16 and 21 years. <a href="/wiki/Demeny_voting" title="Demeny voting">Demeny voting</a> has been proposed as a form of proxy voting by parents on behalf of their children who are below the age of suffrage. The movement to lower the voting age is one aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Youth_rights" title="Youth rights">Youth rights</a> movement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminality">Criminality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criminality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement#Resulting_from_criminal_conviction" title="Disfranchisement">Disfranchisement § Resulting from criminal conviction</a></div>
<p>Some countries restrict the voting rights of convicted criminals. Some countries, and <a href="/wiki/Felony_disenfranchisement_in_the_United_States" title="Felony disenfranchisement in the United States">some U.S. states</a>, also deny the right to vote to those convicted of serious crimes even after they are released from prison. In some cases (e.g. in <a href="/wiki/Felony_disenfranchisement_in_the_United_States" title="Felony disenfranchisement in the United States">many U.S. states</a>) the denial of the right to vote is automatic upon a felony conviction; in other cases (e.g. France and Germany) deprivation of the vote is meted out separately, and often limited to perpetrators of specific crimes such as those against the electoral system or corruption of public officials. In the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, prisoners are allowed the right to vote, following <a href="/wiki/Hirst_v_United_Kingdom_(No_2)" title="Hirst v United Kingdom (No 2)">the <i>Hirst v UK (No2)</i> ruling</a>, which was granted in 2006. <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> allowed only prisoners serving a term of less than 2 years the right to vote, but this was found to be unconstitutional in 2002 by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauv%C3%A9_v._Canada_(Chief_Electoral_Officer)" title="Sauvé v. Canada (Chief Electoral Officer)">Sauvé v. Canada (Chief Electoral Officer)</a></i>, and all prisoners have been allowed to vote as of the <a href="/wiki/2004_Canadian_federal_election" title="2004 Canadian federal election">2004 Canadian federal election</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Residency">Residency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Residency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under certain electoral systems elections are held within subnational jurisdictions, thus preventing persons from voting who would otherwise be eligible on the basis that they do not reside within such a jurisdiction, or because they live in an area that cannot participate. In the United States, license plates in Washington, D.C. read "TAXATION WITHOUT REPRESENTATION," in reference to the district not holding a seat in either the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> or <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>, however residents can vote in presidential elections based on the Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution adopted in 1961. Residents of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> enjoy neither.
</p><p>Sometimes citizens become ineligible to vote because they are no longer resident in their country of citizenship. For example, Australian citizens who have been outside Australia for more than one and fewer than six years may excuse themselves from the requirement to vote in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_electoral_system" title="Australian electoral system">Australian elections</a> while they remain outside Australia (voting in Australia is compulsory for resident citizens).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup>
Danish citizens that reside permanently outside Denmark lose their right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cases, a certain period of residence in a locality may required for the right to vote in that location. For example, in the United Kingdom up to 2001, each 15 February a new electoral register came into effect, based on registration as of the previous 10 October, with the effect of limiting voting to those resident five to seventeen months earlier depending on the timing of the election.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationality">Nationality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Nationality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Right_of_foreigners_to_vote" title="Right of foreigners to vote">Right of foreigners to vote</a></div>
<p>In most countries, suffrage is limited to citizens and, in many cases, permanent residents of that country. However, some members of supra-national organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and the European Union have granted voting rights to citizens of all countries within that organisation. Until the mid-twentieth century, many Commonwealth countries gave the vote to all British citizens within the country, regardless of whether they were normally resident there. In most cases this was because there was no distinction between <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British and local citizenship</a>. Several countries qualified this with restrictions preventing non-white British citizens such as Indians and British Africans from voting. Under European Union law, citizens of European Union countries can vote in each other's local and European Parliament elections on the same basis as citizens of the country in question, but usually not in national elections.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naturalization">Naturalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Naturalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some countries, naturalized citizens do not have the right to vote or to be a candidate, either permanently or for a determined period.
</p><p>Article 5 of the 1831 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Belgium" title="Constitution of Belgium">Belgian Constitution</a> made a difference between ordinary naturalization, and <i>grande naturalisation</i>. Only (former) foreigners who had been granted <i>grande naturalisation</i> were entitled to vote, be a candidate for parliamentary elections, or be appointed minister. However, ordinary naturalized citizens could vote for municipal elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> Ordinary naturalized citizens and citizens who had acquired Belgian nationality through marriage could vote, but not run as candidates for parliamentary elections in 1976. The concepts of ordinary and grande naturalization were suppressed from the Constitution in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the 1889 Nationality Law barred those who had acquired the French nationality by naturalization or marriage from voting, and from eligibility and access to several public jobs. In 1938 the delay was reduced to five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> These instances of discrimination, as well as others against naturalized citizens, were gradually abolished in 1973 (9 January 1973 law) and 1983.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, a former French <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a>, and in Guinea, a former French colony, naturalized citizens are prohibited from voting for five years following their naturalization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Federated States of Micronesia</a>, one must be a Micronesian citizen for at least 15 years to run for parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, only citizens by birth are eligible for being elected to the national legislature; naturalized citizens enjoy only voting rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>, naturalized citizens have the right of eligibility to the parliament after five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the President and <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> must be natural-born citizens. All other governmental offices may be held by any citizen, although citizens may only run for Congress after an extended period of citizenship (seven years for the House of Representatives and nine for the Senate).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In France, an 1872 law, rescinded by a 1945 decree, prohibited all army personnel from voting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Ireland, police (the <a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda Síochána">Garda Síochána</a> and, before 1925, the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Metropolitan_Police" title="Dublin Metropolitan Police">Dublin Metropolitan Police</a>) were barred from voting in national elections, though not <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Local government in the Republic of Ireland">local elections</a>, from 1923 to 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1876 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Texas" title="Constitution of Texas">Constitution of Texas</a> (article VI, section 1) stated that "The following classes of persons shall not be allowed to vote in this State, to wit: (…) Fifth—All soldiers, marines and seamen, employed in the service of the army or navy of the United States."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many countries with a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential system</a> of government a person is forbidden to be a legislator and an official of the executive branch at the same time. Such provisions are found, for example, in Article I of the U.S. Constitution.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_around_the_world">History around the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: History around the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage#Dates_by_country" title="Universal suffrage">Universal suffrage § Dates by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_women%27s_suffrage" title="Timeline of women's suffrage">Timeline of women's suffrage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_States" title="Timeline of women's suffrage in the United States">Timeline of women's suffrage in the United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Voting_age" title="Voting age">Voting age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg/350px-Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg/525px-Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg/700px-Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universal_suffrage_granted_to_women,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with universal suffrage granted to women, 2017<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1840, the Kingdom of Hawai'i adopted full suffrage for all subjects without mention of sex, but the constitution of 1852 specified voting by male subjects over the age of 20. In 1902 the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Franchise_Act_1902" title="Commonwealth Franchise Act 1902">Commonwealth Franchise Act</a> enabled women to vote federally in Australia and in the state of New South Wales. This legislation also allowed women to run for government, making Australia the first in the world to allow this. In 1906 Finland became the next nation in the world to give all adult citizens full suffrage, in other words the right to vote and to run for office. New Zealand granted all adult citizens the right to vote (in 1893), but women did not get the right to run for the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_House_of_Representatives" title="New Zealand House of Representatives">New Zealand legislature</a> until 1919.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Suffrage_in_Australia" title="Suffrage in Australia">Suffrage in Australia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_rights_of_Australian_Aboriginals" title="Voting rights of Australian Aboriginals">Voting rights of Australian Aboriginals</a></div>
<ul><li>1855 – South Australia is the first colony to allow all male suffrage to British subjects (later extended to <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians" title="Aboriginal Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a> over the age of 21.</li>
<li>1894 – South Australian women eligible to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aec_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-aec-78">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>1896 – Tasmania becomes last colony to allow all male suffrage.</li>
<li>1899 – Western Australian women eligible to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aec_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-aec-78">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>1902 – The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Franchise_Act_1902" title="Commonwealth Franchise Act 1902">Commonwealth Franchise Act</a> enables women to vote federally and in the state of New South Wales. This legislation also allows women to run for government, making Australia the first democratic state in the world to allow this.</li>
<li>1921 – <a href="/wiki/Edith_Cowan" title="Edith Cowan">Edith Cowan</a> is elected to the West Australian Legislative Assembly as member for West Perth, the first woman elected to any Australian Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>1962 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aborigines" title="Australian Aborigines">Australian Aborigines</a> guaranteed the right to vote in Commonwealth elections, however, in practice this right was dependent on Aboriginal voting rights having been granted by the individual's respective state.</li>
<li>1965 – Queensland is the last state to grant voting rights to Aboriginal Australians.</li>
<li>1973 - After South Australian Premier <a href="/wiki/Don_Dunstan" title="Don Dunstan">Don Dunstan</a> introduced the Age of Majority (Reduction) Bill in October 1970, the voting age in South Australia was lowered to 18 years old in 1973. Consequently, the voting age for all federal elections was lowered from 21 to 18. The states had lowered the voting age to 18 by 1973, the first being Western Australia in 1970.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil">Brazil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Brazil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1824 – The <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Constitution_of_1824" title="Brazilian Constitution of 1824">first Brazilian constitution</a> allows free men over the age of 25 to vote, even former slaves, but there are income restrictions. The Chamber of Deputies' representatives are chosen via electoral colleges.</li>
<li>1881 – The <a href="/wiki/Saraiva_Law" title="Saraiva Law">Saraiva Law</a> implements direct voting, but there are literacy restrictions. Women and slaves do not have the right to vote.</li>
<li>1932 – Voting becomes obligatory for all adults over 21 years of age, regardless of gender or income.</li>
<li>1955 – Adoption of standardized voting ballots and further identification requirements to mitigate electoral frauds.</li>
<li>1964 – Military dictatorship established. From then on, presidents were elected by members of the congress, chosen by regular vote.</li>
<li>1989 – Reestablishment of universal suffrage for all citizens over 16 years of age. People considered illiterate are not obliged to vote, nor are people younger than 18 and older than 70 years of age. People under the obligation rule shall file a document to justify their absence should they not vote.</li>
<li>2000 – Brazil becomes the first country to fully adopt electronic ballots in their voting process.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Famous_Five_(Canada)" title="The Famous Five (Canada)">The Famous Five (Canada)</a></div>
<ul><li>1871 – One of the first acts of the new Province of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> strips the franchise from First Nations, and ensures Chinese and Japanese people are prevented from voting.</li>
<li>1916 – <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> becomes the first province in which women have the right to vote in provincial elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>1917 – <a href="/wiki/Wartime_Elections_Act" title="Wartime Elections Act">Wartime Elections Act</a> gives voting rights to women with relatives fighting overseas. Voting rights are stripped from all "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enemy_aliens" title="Enemy aliens">enemy aliens</a>" (those born in enemy countries who arrived in Canada after 1902; see also <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Canadian_internment" title="Ukrainian Canadian internment">Ukrainian Canadian internment</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Military_Voters_Act" title="Military Voters Act">Military Voters Act</a> gives the vote to all soldiers, even non-citizens, (with the exception of Indian and Metis veterans)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> and to women serving as nurses or clerks for the armed forces, but the votes are not for specific candidates but simply for or against the government.</li>
<li>1918 – Women gain full voting rights in federal elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faculty.marianopolis.edu_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-faculty.marianopolis.edu-84">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>1919 – Women gain the right to run for federal office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faculty.marianopolis.edu_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-faculty.marianopolis.edu-84">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>1940 – Quebec becomes the last province where women's right to vote is recognized. (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_women_during_the_world_wars" title="Canadian women during the world wars">Canadian women during the world wars</a> for more information on Canadian suffrage)</li>
<li>1947 – Racial exclusions against Chinese and Indo-Canadians lifted.</li>
<li>1948 – Racial exclusions against Japanese Canadians lifted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>1955 – Religious exclusions are removed from election laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-86">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>1960 – Right to vote is extended unconditionally to <a href="/wiki/First_Nations_in_Canada" title="First Nations in Canada">First Nations</a> peoples. (Previously they could vote only by giving up their status as First Nations people.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FN_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FN-87">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>1960 – Right to vote in advance is extended to all electors willing to swear they would be absent on election day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>1965 – <a href="/wiki/First_Nations_in_Canada" title="First Nations in Canada">First Nations</a> people granted the right to vote in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a> provincial elections, starting with the <a href="/wiki/1967_Alberta_general_election" title="1967 Alberta general election">1967 Alberta general election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FN_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FN-87">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>1969 – <a href="/wiki/First_Nations_in_Canada" title="First Nations in Canada">First Nations</a> people granted the right to vote in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> provincial elections, starting with the <a href="/wiki/1970_Quebec_general_election" title="1970 Quebec general election">1970 Quebec general election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FN_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-FN-87">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>1970 – Voting age lowered from 21 to 18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>1982 – <i><a href="/wiki/Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a></i> guarantees all adult citizens the right to vote.</li>
<li>1988 – Supreme Court of Canada rules mentally ill patients have the right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li>1993<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-86">[84]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> – Any elector can vote in advance.</li>
<li>2000 – Legislation is introduced making it easier for people of <a href="/wiki/No_fixed_abode" title="No fixed abode">no fixed address</a> to vote.</li>
<li>2002 – Prisoners given the right to vote in the riding (voting district) where they were convicted.
