All adult Canadians except the Chief and Deputy <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Electoral_Officer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Electoral Officer (page does not exist)">Electoral Officers</a> can now vote in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li>2019 – The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a> rules that portions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Canada_Elections_Act" title="Canada Elections Act">Canada Elections Act</a></i> which prevent citizens who have been living abroad for more than five years from voting by mail are in violation of <a href="/wiki/Section_3_of_the_Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Section 3 of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Section 3 of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a> and thus unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The European Union has given the right to vote in municipal elections to the citizen of another EU country by the Council Directive 94/80/EG from 19 December 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1906 – Full suffrage for all citizens adults aged 24 or older at beginning of voting year.</li>
<li>1921 – Suppression of <a href="/wiki/Plural_voting" title="Plural voting">property-based number of votes</a> on municipal level; equal vote for everybody.</li>
<li>1944 – Voting age lowered to 21 years.</li>
<li>1969 – Voting age lowered to 20 years.</li>
<li>1972 – Voting age lowered to 18 years.</li>
<li>1981 – Voting and eligibility rights were granted to <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Passport_Union" title="Nordic Passport Union">Nordic Passport Union</a> country citizens without residency condition for <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Finland" title="Municipalities of Finland">municipal</a> elections.</li>
<li>1991 – Voting and eligibility rights were extended to all foreign residents in 1991 with a two-year residency condition for <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Finland" title="Municipalities of Finland">municipal</a> elections.</li>
<li>1995 – Residency requirement abolished for EU residents, in conformity with European legislation (Law 365/95, confirmed by Electoral Law 714/1998).</li>
<li>1996 – Voting age lowered to 18 years at date of voting.</li>
<li>2000 – Section 14, al. 2 of the 2000 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Finland" title="Constitution of Finland">Constitution of Finland</a> states that "<i>Every Finnish citizen and every foreigner permanently resident in Finland, having attained eighteen years of age, has the right to vote in municipal elections and municipal referendums, as provided by an Act. Provisions on the right to otherwise participate in municipal government are laid down by an Act.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>11 August 1792 : Introduction of universal suffrage (men only)</li>
<li>1795 : Universal suffrage for men is replaced with indirect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Census_suffrage" title="Census suffrage">Census suffrage</a></li>
<li>13 December 1799: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consulat" title="Consulat">French Consulate</a> re-establishes male universal suffrage increased from 246,000 to over 9 million.</li>
<li>In 1850 (31 May): The number of people eligible to vote is reduced by 30% by excluding criminals and the homeless.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> calls a referendum in 1851 (21 December), all men aged 21 and over are allowed to vote. Male universal suffrage is established thereafter.</li>
<li>As of 21 April 1944 the franchise is extended to women over 21.</li>
<li>On 5 July 1974 the minimum age to vote is reduced to 18 years old.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Parliament" title="Frankfurt Parliament">1848</a> – male citizens (citizens of state in German Confederation), adult and "independent" got voting rights, male voting population - 85%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>1849 – male citizens above 25, not disfranchised, not declared legally incapable, didn't claim pauper relief a year before the election, not a bankrupt nor in bankruptcy proceedings, not convicted of electoral fraud,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>1866 – male citizens above 25 (citizen for at least 3 years), not disfranchised, not declared legally incapable, didn't claim pauper relief a year before the election, enrolled on the electoral roll, inhabitant of the electoral district,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>1869 – male citizens above 25 (citizens of state in North German Confederation), not disfranchised, not a bankrupt nor in bankruptcy proceedings, not serving soldier, didn't claim pauper relief a year before the election, inhabitant of the electoral district, not in prison, not declared legally incapable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>1918 - full suffrage for all citizens above 20<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li>1970 - full suffrage for all citizens above 18<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>2019 - suffrage for citizens with <a href="/wiki/Insanity_defense" title="Insanity defense">insanity defense</a>, and persons under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guardianship" title="Guardianship">guardianship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span id="Kingdom_of_Hawai.27i"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Hawai'i">Kingdom of Hawai'i</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Hawai'i">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1840, the king of Hawai'i issued a constitution that granted universal suffrage without mention of sex or age, but later amendments added restrictions, as the influence of Caucasian settlers increased:
</p>
<ul><li>1852 – Women lost the right to vote, and the minimum voting age was specified as 20.</li>
<li>1864 – Voting was restricted on the basis of new qualifications—literacy and either a certain level of income or property ownership.</li>
<li>1887 – Citizens of Hawai'i with Asian descent were disqualified. There was an increase in the minimum value of income or owned property.</li></ul>
<p>Hawai'i lost its independence in 1893.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong">Hong Kong</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Hong Kong">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Minimum age to vote was reduced from 21 to 18 years in <a href="/wiki/1995_Hong_Kong_legislative_election" title="1995 Hong Kong legislative election">1995</a>. The Basic Law, the constitution of the territory since 1997, stipulates that all permanent residents (a status conferred by birth or by seven years of residence) have the right to vote. The right of permanent residents who have right of abode in other countries to stand in election is, however, restricted to 12 <a href="/wiki/Functional_constituency_(Hong_Kong)" title="Functional constituency (Hong Kong)">functional constituencies</a> by the Legislative Council Ordinance of 1997.
</p><p>The right to vote and the right to stand in elections are not equal. Fewer than 250,000 of the electorate are eligible to run in the 30 functional constituencies, of which 23 are elected by fewer than 80,000 of the electorate, and in the 2008 Legislative Council election 14 members were elected unopposed from these functional constituencies. The size of the electorates of some constituencies is fewer than 200. Only persons who can demonstrate a connection to the sector are eligible to run in a functional constituency.
</p><p>The Legislative Council (Amendment) Bill 2012, if passed, amends the Legislative Council Ordinance to restrict the right to stand in Legislative Council by-elections in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographical_constituencies" title="Geographical constituencies">geographical constituencies</a> and the District Council (Second) functional constituency. In addition to those persons who are mentally disabled, bankrupt, or imprisoned, members who resign their seats will not have the right to stand for six months' time from their resignation. The bill is currently passing through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_Whole" title="Committee of the Whole">committee stage</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Hungary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1848 - The parliament of the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1848" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1848">Hungarian Revolution of 1848</a> introduced voting rights to men over 20 who met certain criteria as part of the legislative package known as the <a href="/wiki/April_Laws" title="April Laws">April Laws</a>.</li>
<li>1874 - The reintroduction of suffrage following the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Compromise_of_1867" title="Austro-Hungarian Compromise of 1867">Compromise of 1867</a> changed some of the criteria, for instance moving from a wealth based threshold of eligibility to a tax based threshold.</li>
<li>1918 - Universal suffrage for those over 24 who can read and write. While this law introduced voting rights for women they could not exercise this right for some years due to the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Hungarian Soviet Republic">revolution of 1919</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the very first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_general_election,_1951%E2%80%9352" title="Indian general election, 1951–52">Indian general election held in 1951–52</a>, universal suffrage for all adult citizens aged 21 or older was established under Article 326 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution of India</a>. The minimum voting age was reduced to 18 years by the <a href="/wiki/Sixty-first_Amendment_of_the_Constitution_of_India" title="Sixty-first Amendment of the Constitution of India">61st Amendment</a>, effective 28 March 1989.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_franchise_in_Ireland" title="History of the franchise in Ireland">History of the franchise in Ireland</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isle_of_Man">Isle of Man</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Isle of Man">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1866 – The House of Keys Election Act makes the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Keys" title="House of Keys">House of Keys</a> an elected body. The vote is given to men over the age of 21 who own property worth at least £8 a year or rent property worth at least £12 a year. Candidates must be male, with real estate of an annual value of £100, or of £50 along with a personal estate producing an annual income of £100.</li>
<li>1881 – The House of Keys Election Act is amended so that the property qualification is reduced to a net annual value of not less than £4.  Most significantly, the Act is also amended to extend the franchise to unmarried women and widows over the age of 21 who own property, making the Isle of Man the first place to give some women the vote in a national election. The property qualification for candidates is modified to allow the alternative of personal property producing a year income of £150.</li>
<li>1892 – The franchise is extended to unmarried women and widows over the age of 21 who rent property worth a net annual value of at least £4, as well as to male lodgers. The property qualification for candidates is removed.</li>
<li>1903 – A residency qualification is introduced in addition to the property qualification for voters. The time between elections is reduced from 7 to 5 years.</li>
<li>1919 – Universal adult suffrage based on residency is introduced: all male and female residents over the age of 21 may vote. The entire electorate (with the exception of clergy and holders of office of profit) becomes eligible to stand for election.</li>
<li>1970 – Voting age lowered to 18.</li>
<li>2006 – Voting age lowered to 16. The age of eligibility for candidates remains at 18.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Supreme Court states that "the rules derogating from the passive electoral law must be strictly interpreted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suffrage_in_Japan" title="Suffrage in Japan">Suffrage in Japan</a></div>
<ul><li>1889 – Male taxpayers above 25 that paid at least 15 JPY of tax got voting rights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> the voting population were 450,000 (1,1% of Japan population),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1900 – Male taxpayers above 25 that paid at least 10 JPY of tax got voting rights, the voting population were 980,000 (2,2% of Japan population),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1919 – Male taxpayers above 25 that paid at least 3 JPY of tax got voting rights, the voting population were 3,070,000 (5,5% of Japan population)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-106">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>1925 – Male above 25 got voting rights, the voting population were 12,410,000 (20% of Japan population),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1945 – Japan citizens above 20 got voting rights, the voting population were 36,880,000 (48,7% of Japan population),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-106">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>2015 – Japan citizens above 18 got voting rights, voting population - 83,3% of Japan population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_voting_in_New_Zealand" title="History of voting in New Zealand">History of voting in New Zealand</a></div>
<ul><li>1853 – British government passes the <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Constitution_Act_1852" title="New Zealand Constitution Act 1852">New Zealand Constitution Act 1852</a>, granting limited self-rule, including a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral_parliament" title="Bicameral parliament">bicameral parliament</a>, to the colony. The vote was limited to male British subjects aged 21 or over who owned or rented sufficient property and were not imprisoned for a serious offence. Communally owned land was excluded from the property qualification, thus disenfranchising most <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> (indigenous) men.</li>
<li>1860 – Franchise extended to holders of miner's licenses who met all voting qualifications except that of property.</li>
<li>1867 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_seats" title="Māori seats">Māori seats</a> established, giving Māori four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reserved_seats" title="Reserved seats">reserved seats</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lower_house" title="Lower house">lower house</a>. There was no property qualification; thus Māori men gained universal suffrage before other New Zealanders. The number of seats did not reflect the size of the Māori population, but Māori men who met the property requirement for general electorates were able to vote in them or in the Māori electorates but not both.</li>
<li>1879 – Property requirement abolished.</li>
<li>1893 – <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_New_Zealand" title="Women's suffrage in New Zealand">Women won equal voting rights</a> with men, making New Zealand the first nation in the world to allow women to vote.</li>
<li>1969 – Voting age lowered to 20.</li>
<li>1974 – Voting age lowered to 18.</li>
<li>1975 – Franchise extended to permanent residents of New Zealand, regardless of whether they have citizenship.</li>
<li>1996 – Number of Māori seats increased to reflect Māori population.</li>
<li>2010 – Prisoners imprisoned for one year or more denied voting rights while serving the sentence.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Norway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1814 – The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Norwegian constitution</a> gave male landowners or officials above the age of 25 full voting rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>1885 – Male taxpayers that paid at least 500 <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_krone" title="Norwegian krone">NOK</a> of tax (800 NOK in towns) got voting rights.</li>
<li>1900 – Universal suffrage for men over 25.</li>
<li>1901 – Women, over 25, paying tax or having common household with a man paying tax, got the right to vote in local elections.</li>
<li>1909 – Women, over 25, paying tax or having common household with a man paying tax, got full voting rights.</li>
<li>1913 – Universal suffrage for all over 25, applying from the election in 1915.</li>
<li>1920 – Voting age lowered to 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>1946 – Voting age lowered to 21.</li>
<li>1967 – Voting age lowered to 20.</li>
<li>1978 – Voting age lowered to 18.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poland">Poland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Poland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1918 – In its first days of independence in 1918, after 123 years of partition, voting rights were granted to both men and women. Eight women were elected to the <a href="/wiki/Sejm" title="Sejm">Sejm</a> in 1919.</li>
<li>1952 – Voting age lowered to 18.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Voting_rights_in_Singapore" title="Voting rights in Singapore">Voting rights in Singapore</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1910 – The <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> is established by the <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_Act_1909" title="South Africa Act 1909">South Africa Act 1909</a>. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Assembly_(South_Africa)" title="House of Assembly (South Africa)">House of Assembly</a> is elected by <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first-past-the-post</a> voting in single-member constituencies. The franchise qualifications are the same as those previously existing for elections of the legislatures of the colonies that comprised the Union. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State_Province" title="Orange Free State Province">Orange Free State</a> the franchise is limited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_South_African" title="White South African">white</a> men. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natal_Province" title="Natal Province">Natal</a> the franchise is limited to men meeting property and literacy qualifications; it was theoretically colour-blind but in practise nearly all non-white men were excluded. The traditional "<a href="/wiki/Cape_Qualified_Franchise" title="Cape Qualified Franchise">Cape Qualified Franchise</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a> is limited to men meeting property and literacy qualifications and is colour-blind; nonetheless 85% of voters are white. The rights of non-white voters in the Cape Province are protected by an <a href="/wiki/Entrenched_clause" title="Entrenched clause">entrenched clause</a> in the South Africa Act requiring a two-thirds vote in a joint sitting of both Houses of Parliament.</li>
<li>1930 – The <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Enfranchisement_Act,_1930" title="Women's Enfranchisement Act, 1930">Women's Enfranchisement Act, 1930</a> extends the right to vote to all white women over the age of 21.</li>
<li>1931 – The <a href="/wiki/Franchise_Laws_Amendment_Act,_1931" title="Franchise Laws Amendment Act, 1931">Franchise Laws Amendment Act, 1931</a> removes the property and literacy qualifications for all white men over the age of 21, but they are retained for non-white voters.</li>
<li>1936 – The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_Natives_Act,_1936" title="Representation of Natives Act, 1936">Representation of Natives Act, 1936</a> removes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_African" title="Black African">black</a> voters in the Cape Province from the common voters' roll and instead allows them to elect three "Native Representative Members" to the House of Assembly. Four <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_South_Africa" title="Senate of South Africa">Senators</a> are to be indirectly elected by chiefs and local authorities to represent black South Africans throughout the country. The act is passed with the necessary two-thirds majority in a joint sitting.</li>
<li>1951 – The <a href="/wiki/Separate_Representation_of_Voters_Act,_1951" title="Separate Representation of Voters Act, 1951">Separate Representation of Voters Act, 1951</a> is passed by Parliament by an ordinary majority in separate sittings. It purports to remove <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coloured" title="Coloured">coloured</a> voters in the Cape Province from the common voters' roll and instead allow them to elect four "Coloured Representative Members" to the House of Assembly.</li>
<li>1952 – In <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harris_v_Minister_of_the_Interior" title="Harris v Minister of the Interior">Harris v Minister of the Interior</a></i> the Separate Representation of Voters Act is annulled by the Appellate Division of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_South_Africa" title="Supreme Court of South Africa">Supreme Court</a> because it was not passed with the necessary two-thirds majority in a joint sitting. Parliament passes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Parliament_Act,_1952" title="High Court of Parliament Act, 1952">High Court of Parliament Act, 1952</a>, purporting to allow it to reverse this decision, but the Appellate Division annuls it as well.</li>
<li>1956 – By packing the Senate and the Appellate Division, the government passes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Africa_Act_Amendment_Act,_1956" title="South Africa Act Amendment Act, 1956">South Africa Act Amendment Act, 1956</a>, reversing the annulment of the Separate Representation of Voters Act and giving it the force of law.</li>
<li>1958 – The <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Law_Amendment_Act,_1958" title="Electoral Law Amendment Act, 1958">Electoral Law Amendment Act, 1958</a> reduces the <a href="/wiki/Voting_age" title="Voting age">voting age</a> for white voters from 21 to 18.</li>
<li>1959 – The <a href="/wiki/Promotion_of_Bantu_Self-government_Act,_1959" title="Promotion of Bantu Self-government Act, 1959">Promotion of Bantu Self-government Act, 1959</a> repeals the Representation of Natives Act, removing all representation of black people in Parliament.</li>
<li>1968 – The <a href="/wiki/Separate_Representation_of_Voters_Amendment_Act,_1968" title="Separate Representation of Voters Amendment Act, 1968">Separate Representation of Voters Amendment Act, 1968</a> repeals the Separate Representation of Voters Act, removing all representation of coloured people in Parliament.</li>
<li>1969 – The first election of the <a href="/wiki/Coloured_Persons_Representative_Council" title="Coloured Persons Representative Council">Coloured Persons Representative Council</a> (CPRC), which has limited legislative powers, is held. Every Coloured citizen over the age of 21 can vote for its members, in first-past-the-post elections in single-member constituencies.</li>
<li>1978 – The voting age for the CPRC is reduced from 21 to 18.</li>
<li>1981 – The first election of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Indian_Council" title="South African Indian Council">South African Indian Council</a> (SAIC), which has limited legislative powers, is held. Every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_South_African" title="Indian South African">Indian South African</a> citizen over the age of 18 can vote for its members, in first-past-the-post elections in single-member constituencies.</li>
<li>1984 – The <a href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1983" title="South African Constitution of 1983">Constitution of 1983</a> establishes the <a href="/wiki/Tricameral_Parliament" title="Tricameral Parliament">Tricameral Parliament</a>. Two new Houses of Parliament are created, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(South_Africa)" title="House of Representatives (South Africa)">House of Representatives</a> to represent coloured citizens and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Delegates_(South_Africa)" title="House of Delegates (South Africa)">House of Delegates</a> to represent Indian citizens. Every coloured and Indian citizen over the age of 18 can vote in elections for the relevant house. As with the House of Assembly, the members are elected by first-past-the-post voting in single-member constituencies. The CPRC and SAIC are abolished.</li>
<li>1994 – With the end of <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_Constitution_of_1993" title="South African Constitution of 1993">Interim Constitution of 1993</a> abolishes the Tricameral Parliament and all racial discrimination in voting rights. A new <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_South_Africa" title="National Assembly of South Africa">National Assembly</a> is created, and every South African citizen over the age of 18 has the right to vote for the assembly. The right to vote is also extended to long term residents. It is estimated the 500 000 foreign nationals voted in the 1994 national and provincial elections. Elections of the assembly are based on <a href="/wiki/Party-list_proportional_representation" title="Party-list proportional representation">party-list proportional representation</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_to_vote" title="Right to vote">right to vote</a> is enshrined in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_(South_Africa)" title="Bill of Rights (South Africa)">Bill of Rights</a>.</li>
<li>1999 – In <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=August_and_Another_v_Electoral_Commission_and_Others&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="August and Another v Electoral Commission and Others (page does not exist)">August and Another v Electoral Commission and Others</a></i> the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_South_Africa" title="Constitutional Court of South Africa">Constitutional Court</a> rules that prisoners cannot be denied the right to vote without a law that explicitly does so.</li>
<li>2003 – The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Electoral_Laws_Amendment_Act,_2003&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Electoral Laws Amendment Act, 2003 (page does not exist)">Electoral Laws Amendment Act, 2003</a> purports to prohibit convicted prisoners from voting.</li>
<li>2004 – In <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Minister_of_Home_Affairs_v_NICRO_and_Others&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Minister of Home Affairs v NICRO and Others (page does not exist)">Minister of Home Affairs v NICRO and Others</a></i> the Constitutional Court rules that prisoners cannot be denied the right to vote, and invalidates the laws that do so.</li>
<li>2009 – In <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Richter_v_Minister_for_Home_Affairs_and_Others&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Richter v Minister for Home Affairs and Others (page does not exist)">Richter v Minister for Home Affairs and Others</a></i> the Constitutional Court rules that South African citizens outside the country cannot be denied the right to vote.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>1809 – New <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> adopted and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Separation_of_power" title="Separation of power">separation of powers</a> outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government_(1809)" title="Instrument of Government (1809)">Instrument of Government</a>.</li>
<li>1810 – The <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Parliament_Act_of_1810" title="Swedish Parliament Act of 1810">Riksdag Act</a>, setting out the procedures of functioning of the <a href="/wiki/Riksdag" title="Riksdag">Riksdag</a>, is introduced.</li>
<li>1862 – Under the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_municipal_reforms_of_1862" title="Swedish municipal reforms of 1862">municipal laws of 1862</a>, some women were entitled to vote in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_elections" title="Local elections">local elections</a>.</li>
<li>1865 – <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Parliament_of_Four_Estates&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Parliament of Four Estates (page does not exist)">Parliament of Four Estates</a> abolished and replaced by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a>. The members of the First Chamber were elected indirectly by the county councils and the municipal assemblies in the larger towns and cities.</li>
<li>1909 – All men who had done their <a href="/wiki/Military_service" title="Military service">military service</a> and who paid <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">tax</a> were granted suffrage.</li>
<li>1918 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Suffrage" title="Universal Suffrage">Universal</a>, and <a href="#Equal_suffrage">equal</a> suffrage were introduced for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_elections" title="Local elections">local elections</a>.</li>
<li>1919 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Suffrage" title="Universal Suffrage">Universal</a>, <a href="#Equal_suffrage">equal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> granted for general elections.</li>
<li>1921 – First <a href="/wiki/1921_Swedish_general_election" title="1921 Swedish general election">general election</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Suffrage" title="Universal Suffrage">universal</a>, <a href="#Equal_suffrage">equal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> enacted, although some groups were still unable to vote.</li>
<li>1922 – Requirement that men had to have completed national <a href="/wiki/Military_service" title="Military service">military service</a> to be able to vote abolished.</li>
<li>1937 – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intern" title="Intern">Interns</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">prisons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychiatric_hospital" title="Psychiatric hospital">institutions</a> granted suffrage.</li>
<li>1945 – Individuals who had gone into <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy" title="Bankruptcy">bankruptcy</a> or were dependent on <a href="/wiki/Welfare" title="Welfare">welfare</a> granted suffrage.</li>
<li>1970 – Indirectly elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_chamber" title="Upper chamber">upper chamber</a> dismantled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (May 2018)">relevant?</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>1974 – <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government_(1809)" title="Instrument of Government (1809)">Instrument of Government</a> stopped being enforced.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:PCR" title="Wikipedia:PCR"><span title="The material near this tag may lack sufficient context (May 2018)">needs context</span></a></i>]</sup>.</li>
<li>1989 – The final limitations on suffrage abolished along with the <a href="/wiki/Riksdag" title="Riksdag">Riksdag</a>'s decision to abolish the 'declaration of legal incompetency'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkey">Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Turkish_politics" title="Women in Turkish politics">Women in Turkish politics</a></div>
<ul><li>1926 – <a href="/wiki/Turkish_civil_code_(1926)" title="Turkish civil code (1926)">Turkish civil code</a> (Equality in civil rights)</li>
<li>1930 – Right to vote in local elections</li>
<li>1933 – First woman muhtar (Village head) <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BClk%C4%B1z_%C3%9Crb%C3%BCl" title="Gülkız Ürbül">Gülkız Ürbül</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karpuzlu" title="Karpuzlu">Demircidere</a> village, <a href="/wiki/Ayd%C4%B1n_Province" title="Aydın Province">Aydın Province</a></li>
<li>1934 – Right to vote in General elections</li>
<li>1935 – First 18 Women MPs in <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">Turkish parliament</a></li>
<li>1950 – First woman city mayor <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCfide_%C4%B0lhan" title="Müfide İlhan">Müfide İlhan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mersin" title="Mersin">Mersin</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom#History" title="Elections in the United Kingdom">Elections in the United Kingdom § History</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_franchise_in_the_United_Kingdom_1885%E2%80%931918" title="Parliamentary franchise in the United Kingdom 1885–1918">Parliamentary franchise in the United Kingdom 1885–1918</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Women's suffrage in the United Kingdom">Women's suffrage in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February,_1839,_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5662" data-file-width="7590" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg/220px-The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg/330px-The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg/440px-The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February%2C_1839%2C_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_national_convention._As_it_met_on_Monday_the_4th_of_February,_1839,_at_the_British_Coffee_House_ca._1839_LCCN2004669356.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartists'</a> National Convention at the British Coffee House in February 1839</div></div></div>
<p>From 1265, a few percent of the adult male population in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> (of which Wales was a <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">full and equal member from 1542</a>) were able to vote in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Parliaments_of_England" title="List of Parliaments of England">parliamentary elections</a> that occurred at irregular intervals to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origins_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origins-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> The franchise for the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> developed separately. King <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI of England</a> established in 1432 that only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forty_shilling_freeholders" title="Forty shilling freeholders">owners of property worth at least forty shillings</a>, a significant sum, were entitled to vote in an English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_constituency" title="County constituency">county constituency</a>. The franchise was restricted to males by custom rather than statute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> Changes were made to the details of the system, but there was no major reform until the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832#The_franchise" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[nb 3]</a></sup> A series of <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act" title="Reform Act">Reform Acts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act" title="Representation of the People Act">Representation of the People Acts</a> followed. In 1918, all men over 21 and some women over 30 won the right to vote, and in 1928 all women over 21 won the right to vote resulting in universal suffrage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">The Laws in Wales Acts 1535</a> – Legislation provides for Welsh representatives in the House of Commons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-118">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">The Acts of Union 1707</a> – Scotland allocated 45 seats in the House of Commons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-118">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li>1729 – Bribery Act passed to address corruption in elections<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-118">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a> – Ireland allocated 100 seats in the House of Commons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_118-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-118">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> – extended voting rights to adult males who rented propertied land of a certain value, so allowing 1 in 7 males in the UK voting rights.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1867" title="Reform Act 1867">Reform Act 1867</a> – extended the franchise to men in urban areas who met a property qualification, so increasing male suffrage.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884" title="Representation of the People Act 1884">Reform Act 1884</a> – addressed imbalances between the boroughs and the countryside; this brought the voting population to 5,500,000, although 40% of males were still disenfranchised because of the property qualification.</li>
<li>Between 1885 and 1918 moves were made by the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_Kingdom#Formation_of_a_national_movement" title="Women's suffrage in the United Kingdom">women's suffrage</a> movement to ensure votes for women. However, the duration of the First World War stopped this reform movement.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> – the consequences of World War I persuaded the government to expand the right to vote, not only for the many men who fought in the war who were disenfranchised, but also for the women who worked in factories, agriculture and elsewhere as part of the war effort, often substituting for enlisted men and including dangerous work such as in munitions factories. All men aged 21 and over were given the right to vote. Property restrictions for voting were lifted for men. The local government franchise was extended to include all women over 21, on the same terms as men. Parliamentary Votes were given to 40% of women, with property restrictions and limited to those over 30 years old. This increased the electorate from 7.7 million to 21.4 million with women making up 8.5 million of the electorate. Seven percent of the electorate had more than one vote, either because they owned business property or because they were university graduates. The first election with this system was the <a href="/wiki/1918_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1918 United Kingdom general election">1918 general election</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1928" title="Representation of the People Act 1928">Representation of the People Act 1928</a> – equal suffrage for women and men, with voting possible at 21 with no property restrictions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1948" title="Representation of the People Act 1948">Representation of the People Act 1948</a> – removed <a href="/wiki/Plural_voting" title="Plural voting">plural voting</a> in parliamentary elections for university graduates and business owners.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1969" title="Representation of the People Act 1969">Representation of the People Act 1969</a> – extension of suffrage to those 18 and older, and abolition of plural voting in local government elections.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Voting_rights_in_the_United_States" title="Voting rights in the United States">Voting rights in the United States</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a> did not originally define who was eligible to vote, allowing each state to decide this status. In the early history of the U.S., most states allowed only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">white</a> male adult property owners to vote (about 6% of the population).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup> By 1856 property ownership requirements were eliminated in all states, giving suffrage to most white men. However, tax-paying requirements remained in five states until 1860 and in two states until the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBER_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBER-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, five amendments to the Constitution were expressly addressed to the "right to vote"; these amendments limit the basis upon which the right to vote in any U.S. state or other jurisdiction may be abridged or denied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[nb 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">15th Amendment</a> (1870): "The right of citizens of the United States to vote shall not be denied or abridged by the United States or by any State on account of race, color, or previous condition of servitude."</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Nineteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">19th Amendment</a> (1920): "The right of citizens of the United States to vote shall not be denied or abridged by the United States or by any State on account of sex."</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twenty-third_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution">23rd Amendment</a> (1961): provides that residents of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> can vote for the President and Vice President.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twenty-fourth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution">24th Amendment</a> (1964): "The right of citizens of the United States to vote in any primary or other election for President or Vice President, for electors for President or Vice President, or for Senator or Representative in Congress, shall not be denied or abridged by the United States or any State by reason of failure to pay any poll tax or other tax."</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-sixth Amendment to the United States Constitution">26th Amendment</a> (1971): "The right of citizens of the United States, who are eighteen years of age or older, to vote shall not be denied or abridged by the United States or by any State on account of age."</li></ul>
<p>Full removal of racial disenfranchisement of citizens was not secured until the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a> gained passage through <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a>. For state elections, it was not until the U.S. Supreme Court ruled 6–3 in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harper_v._Virginia_Board_of_Elections" title="Harper v. Virginia Board of Elections">Harper v. Virginia Board of Elections</a></i> (1966) that all state poll taxes were declared unconstitutional as violating the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Protection_Clause" title="Equal Protection Clause">Equal Protection Clause</a> of the Fourteenth Amendment. This removed a burden on the poor, including some poor whites who had been disenfranchised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scher2015_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scher2015-124">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHLTS2009_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHLTS2009-125">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Majority-Muslim_countries">Majority-Muslim countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Suffrage&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Majority-Muslim countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Strictly speaking, all ratepayers; however, domestic rates were abolished after the <a href="/wiki/1977_Irish_general_election" title="1977 Irish general election">1977 election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CallananKeogan2003_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-CallananKeogan2003-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example <a href="/wiki/South_Dublin_County_Council" title="South Dublin County Council">South Dublin County Council</a> produced lists of addresses of residences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> and ratepayers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> within <a href="/wiki/Palmerstown" title="Palmerstown">Palmerstown</a> for the 2014 plebiscite on changing the district's spelling.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Until this Act specified 'male persons', a few women had been able to vote in parliamentary elections through property ownership, although this was rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-123">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">14th Amendment</a> (1868) altered the way each state is represented in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>. It counted all residents for apportionment including former slaves, overriding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-fifths_compromise" title="Three-fifths compromise">three-fifths compromise</a> of the original Constitution; it also reduced a state's apportionment if it wrongfully denied the right to vote to males over age 21. However, this sanction was not enforced in practice.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-NBER-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NBER_121-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFStanley_L._Engerman,_University_of_Rochester_and_NBERKenneth_L._Sokoloff,_University_of_California,_Los_Angeles_and_NBER2005">Stanley L. Engerman, University of Rochester and NBER; Kenneth L. Sokoloff, University of California, Los Angeles and NBER (February 2005). <a class="external text" href="http://economics.yale.edu/sites/default/files/files/Workshops-Seminars/Economic-History/sokoloff-050406.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The Evolution of Suffrage Institutions in the New World"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>: 16, 35. <q>By 1840, only three states retained a property qualification, North Carolina (for some state-wide offices only), Rhode Island, and Virginia. In 1856 North Carolina was the last state to end the practice. Tax-paying qualifications were also gone in all but a few states by the Civil War, but they survived into the 20th century in Pennsylvania and Rhode Island.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=The+Evolution+of+Suffrage+Institutions+in+the+New+World&amp;rft.pages=16%2C+35&amp;rft.date=2005-02&amp;rft.au=Stanley+L.+Engerman%2C+University+of+Rochester+and+NBER&amp;rft.au=Kenneth+L.+Sokoloff%2C+University+of+California%2C+Los+Angeles+and+NBER&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Feconomics.yale.edu%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Ffiles%2FWorkshops-Seminars%2FEconomic-History%2Fsokoloff-050406.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASuffrage"></span> <span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_journal" title="Template:Cite journal">cite journal</a>}}</code>: </span><span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment">Cite journal requires <code class="cs1-code">|journal=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#missing_periodical" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span></span>
